import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a parallel associative scan (linear recurrence) over a 1D sequence.
    Given coefficient a and input b at each timestep, computes:
        h_0 = b_0
        h_t = a_t * h_{t-1} + b_t  for t > 0
    This is the core parallelizable primitive underlying Mamba's selective scan and
    other SSM architectures. Sequential in naive PyTorch but can be parallelized
    via the associative scan algorithm in O(L log L) span.

    Args:
        d_model (int): Number of independent recurrence channels.
    """
    def __init__(self, d_model: int):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.d_model = d_model

    def forward(self, a: torch.Tensor, b: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        B, L, D = a.shape
        h = torch.zeros(B, D, device=a.device, dtype=a.dtype)
        outputs = []
        for t in range(L):
            h = a[:, t] * h + b[:, t]
            outputs.append(h)
        return torch.stack(outputs, dim=1)

batch_size = 32
d_model = 256
seq_len = 4096

def get_inputs():
    a = torch.sigmoid(torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, d_model))
    b = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, d_model)
    return [a, b]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [d_model]

That's 32*256=8192 independent scans, each of length 4096. The PyTorch reference does sequential loop in Python, appending to list, which is very slow. Even a simple CUDA kernel that does each scan sequentially within a thread would be much faster because it avoids Python overhead and uses GPU parallelism. But we can do better with a parallel scan within each channel to reduce O(L) to O(log L) steps per channel. However, each scan is only length 4096, and we have 8192 scans. For each thread, memory accesses are coalesced across threads if we store data as (B, L, D) with D as last dimension: a[b, t, d] where d is contiguous. If we launch threads with d as x-dimension and b as y-dimension, then for a fixed t, accesses to a[:, t, :] are contiguous across d. But sequential loop over t means each thread accesses a[:, t, d] for t=0..L-1. The memory layout is (B, L, D). The stride for t is D. Let's analyze the block-level scan approach more carefully. Suppose we have 8192 blocks, each of 256 threads. Each block processes one (b,d). We process the sequence in tiles of 256 elements. For each tile: 1. Each thread loads one a and one b: a[global_idx], b[global_idx]. Store in shared memory. 2. Perform a parallel prefix scan on the 256 elements in shared memory. The associative operator is combine(left, right) = (aR*aL, aR*bL + bR). We want inclusive scan. After step 2, each thread has the cumulative pair for its position within the tile. 3. The thread with the last element (or all threads) compute the tile's aggregate pair (the combination of all elements in the tile). Then we need to apply the prefix from previous tiles to this tile. Let's call the running prefix across tiles `running`. Initially `running` = (1, 0). After processing tile i, the aggregate of tile i is `tile_agg`. The cumulative up to tile i is `tile_cum = combine(running, tile_agg)`? Let's check: `running` is the combined transformation of all previous tiles. For each element in tile i, its total transformation is `combine(running, local_cum)`. Because local_cum combines elements within the tile from left to right. The operator combines left segment then right segment. If `running` represents the transformation of everything before the tile, then for an element at position j in the tile, the total transformation is `running` followed by local_cum_j. So total pair = combine(running, local_cum_j) = (local_a * running.a, local_a * running.b + local_b). Then the output h = total.b. After finishing the tile, we update running = combine(running, tile_agg). So algorithm: shared float s_a[256], s_b[256]; running_a = 1.0f; running_b = 0.0f; for tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; ++tile: idx = tile * 256 + threadIdx.x; s_a[threadIdx.x] = a[idx]; s_b[threadIdx.x] = b[idx]; __syncthreads(); // inclusive scan in s_a, s_b float local_a = s_a[threadIdx.x]; float local_b = s_b[threadIdx.x]; // warp-level scan #pragma unroll for offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2: float temp_a = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_a, offset); float temp_b = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_b, offset); if (lane >= offset): // combine temp (left) and local (right) // local = combine(temp, local) = (local.a * temp.a, local.a * temp.b + local.b) local_a = local_a * temp_a; local_b = local_a * temp_b + local_b; // Wait, we need to be careful: the operator is not commutative. // Let's define combine(left, right) = (right_a * left_a, right_a * left_b + right_b). // For prefix scan, we want to accumulate left to right. So at each step, we combine the value from offset positions to the left (temp) with current (local). // So new_local = combine(temp, local) = (local_a * temp_a, local_a * temp_b + local_b). // This is correct. // After warp scan, local is inclusive scan within warp. // Now need to combine across warps. We can have warp leaders write their warp aggregate to shared memory, then do another scan. // Standard block-level scan: // After warp scan, each lane 31 has warp aggregate. // Write warp_agg to shared memory. // __syncthreads(); // Thread 0 does sequential scan of warp aggregates (there are 8 warps for 256 threads). // __syncthreads(); // Each thread reads the prefix from previous warps and combines. // ... // __syncthreads(); // Then apply running prefix. // Write output. // Compute tile_agg from the last thread (or from the scanned aggregates). // Update running = combine(running, tile_agg). // __syncthreads(); But this is quite complex to get right, especially with the non-commutative operator. Each step accesses shared memory with stride = 1,2,4,...,128. The operator is combine. Since it's not commutative, we need to ensure the order: for inclusive scan, at step k (stride 2^k), each thread reads element at index i - stride and combines it with current. So each block can have, say, 8 warps = 256 threads, processing 8 channels. Or each block can have 1 warp = 32 threads, processing 1 channel. Launching 8192 blocks of 32 threads might have higher block overhead, but it's okay. Let's consider block size = 32, grid size = B*D = 8192. For a given channel (b,d), the warp processes L=4096 elements in chunks of 32. Each thread in the warp handles one element per chunk: thread i handles index chunk*32 + i. Algorithm for a chunk: 1. Load a_val = a[b, chunk*32 + lane, d]; b_val = b[b, chunk*32 + lane, d]; 2. Perform inclusive warp scan on the pairs (a_val, b_val) using warp shuffles. The operator: combine(left, right) = (right.a * left.a, right.a * left.b + right.b). Since we are scanning left to right, we can use the standard shuffle-up approach. For offset = 1,2,4,8,16: temp_a = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, a_val, offset); temp_b = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, b_val, offset); if (lane >= offset): // a_val is current (right), temp is left. // new = combine(temp, a_val) = (a_val * temp_a, a_val * temp_b + b_val) // Wait, we need to store the result back into a_val, b_val as the inclusive prefix up to this lane. b_val = a_val * temp_b + b_val; a_val = a_val * temp_a; After this, a_val = product of a from start of chunk to lane, b_val = corresponding affine term. But note: this only includes elements within the current chunk. It doesn't include the running prefix from previous chunks. 3. After the warp scan, lane 31 has the aggregate of the chunk: chunk_a = a_val (product of all a in chunk), chunk_b = b_val (the b term after applying all a's). We need to broadcast this to all lanes to apply to the next chunk. But first, we need to combine the running prefix from previous chunks with the current chunk's prefix. Let running_a, running_b be the prefix from all previous chunks. Initially running_a = 1, running_b = 0. For each lane i in the warp, the total prefix up to this element is: total_a = chunk_prefix_a[i] * running_a total_b = chunk_prefix_a[i] * running_b + chunk_prefix_b[i] Because running is left, chunk_prefix is right: combine(running, chunk_prefix) = (chunk_a * running_a, chunk_a * running_b + chunk_b). So we can compute output: out_b = total_b (since h = total_b if initial h was 0). Then we update running for the next chunk: running_a = chunk_a * running_a running_b = chunk_a * running_b + chunk_b But chunk_a is the aggregate a of the chunk (product of all a in chunk), chunk_b is the aggregate b (the b term after full chunk). Actually for the chunk aggregate, we need the result of applying the entire chunk to an input. That's exactly what the inclusive scan gives at lane 31 if we started the chunk with identity. However, the warp scan as described above computes inclusive scan of the chunk assuming identity before the first element of the chunk. So lane 31's a_val and b_val are the chunk aggregate. Good. But we need to get chunk_prefix_a[i] and chunk_prefix_b[i] for each lane i. After step 2, a_val and b_val already are the chunk prefix for lane i. Then total_b = a_val * running_b + b_val. Similarly chunk_agg_b. chunk_agg_a = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, a_val, 31); chunk_agg_b = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, b_val, 31); running_b = chunk_agg_a * running_b + chunk_agg_b; running_a = chunk_agg_a * running_a; But wait: running_a is not needed for output (since output is just b). This is an affine map with coefficient a_t and offset b_t. The composition of two affine maps (a1,b1) then (a2,b2) is (a2*a1, a2*b1+b2). So the identity is (1,0). Good. So we need an extra shared memory array for output, or we can reuse the shared memory for a and b since they are no longer needed after the scan. We can reuse s_a or s_b for output. Sequence: 1. Load a tile of a and b into smem_a and smem_b. 2. __syncthreads(); 3. Each warp reads its d's values from smem_a and smem_b. 4. Then they write coalesced. This is beautiful. The only extra cost is two __syncthreads() per tile: one after load, one after write to shared mem before global write. And the warp scan. Total tiles per block: L/32 = 128. So 128 iterations of the loop. Each iteration: - Coalesced load 1024 floats from a, 1024 from b. (2*1024*4=8KB) - 2 __syncthreads() - 32 warps each do warp shuffle scan (5 shuffles each) - 32 warps each do 2 shuffles for broadcast aggregate. - Coalesced write 1024 floats to out. (4KB) Total memory traffic: read a: 134MB, read b: 134MB, write out: 134MB. Plus shared memory traffic (negligible). The loads and stores are fully coalesced. The number of warps is 8192, which should saturate the GPU. This kernel should be near-optimal. Let's compute the FLOPs: each element has 2 FLOPs (mul and add). Total FLOPs = B*L*D*2 = 32*4096*256*2 = 67,108,864. ~67 MFLOPs. That's tiny. So the kernel is purely memory-bound. The memory bandwidth utilization (SOL) should be high because we have coalesced accesses and high occupancy. Now, we need to write the CUDA code. Let's flesh out the kernel. Parameters: - B, L, D are known at runtime. They can be passed to the kernel, or we can compute them from tensor sizes. Tensor shapes: a, b are (B, L, D). out is (B, L, D). Kernel signature: __global__ void scan_kernel(const float* a, const float* b, float* out, int B, int L, int D) Block size: 1024. Grid: dim3(B, D/32). Inside kernel: const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int d_off = tid % 32; const int t_off = tid / 32; const int b = blockIdx.x; const int d_tile = blockIdx.y; const int d = d_tile * 32 + d_off; // Shared memory __shared__ float s_a[32][33]; __shared__ float s_b[32][33]; // We only need two arrays. We can reuse s_a for output. // Actually we need to keep a and b for warp scan? No, warps read from s_a and s_b, compute, then write result back to s_a. // So s_a can be overwritten. float running_h = 0.0f; const int num_tiles = L / 32; // L must be multiple of 32. for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; ++tile) { int t = tile * 32 + t_off; int idx = b * L * D + t * D + d; // Load s_a[d_off][t_off] = a[idx]; s_b[d_off][t_off] = b[idx]; __syncthreads(); // Warp scan int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane = tid % 32; // Each warp handles one d: d_idx = warp_id float a_val = s_a[warp_id][lane]; float b_val = s_b[warp_id][lane]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float temp_a = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, a_val, offset); float temp_b = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, b_val, offset); if (lane >= offset) { b_val = a_val * temp_b + b_val; a_val = a_val * temp_a; } } float out_val = a_val * running_h + b_val; // Write result to shared memory (overwrite s_a) s_a[warp_id][lane] = out_val; // Get chunk aggregate from lane 31 float chunk_agg_a = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, a_val, 31); float chunk_agg_b = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, b_val, 31); running_h = chunk_agg_a * running_h + chunk_agg_b; __syncthreads(); // Write to global memory coalesced out[idx] = s_a[d_off][t_off]; __syncthreads(); // do we need this? Not before next tile's load? The next iteration loads into s_a[d_off][t_off] and s_b[d_off][t_off]. Since all threads have finished reading s_a for output write, and the next loop overwrites s_a, we need a sync to ensure all writes to global memory are done before next load? Actually writes to global memory don't need sync with shared memory, but we need to ensure that all threads have finished writing out before any thread starts the next iteration's load, because the next iteration's load writes to s_a and s_b, and some threads might still be reading s_a in the previous iteration? No, all threads read s_a in the warp scan step before this write-out. After __syncthreads() after writing s_a, all threads can read s_a[d_off][t_off] for global write. After that, the next iteration will write to s_a again. To avoid race between a slow thread still reading s_a for global write and a fast thread writing s_a in the next iteration, we need a sync at the end of the loop. However, the global write reads s_a[d_off][t_off] using tid mapping. The next iteration's load writes s_a[d_off][t_off] using the same tid mapping. So if a thread is still doing the global write while another thread has moved to the next iteration and writes s_a, it's okay because they are writing to the same location (same d_off, t_off) and same thread writes it. Actually each thread writes s_a[d_off][t_off] and later reads it. Since each thread only accesses its own "slot" (determined by tid), there is no inter-thread conflict after the __syncthreads() following the warp write. Wait, after warp scan, each warp writes to s_a[warp_id][lane]. Then __syncthreads(). Then each thread reads s_a[d_off][t_off] and writes to global memory. Here, thread tid reads s_a[d_off][t_off]. This is a different mapping than write: d_off = tid%32, t_off = tid/32. The write mapping was warp_id = tid/32, lane = tid%32. So they are the same! Because warp_id = tid/32, lane = tid%32. And d_off = tid%32 = lane, t_off = tid/32 = warp_id. So s_a[warp_id][lane] is exactly s_a[d_off][t_off]. So each thread writes to the same location it reads. Therefore, after __syncthreads(), each thread can safely read its own s_a slot without any other thread accessing it. Thus no need for another __syncthreads() before the next iteration's load. However, the next iteration's load writes to s_a[d_off][t_off] again. Since each thread overwrites its own slot, and no other thread reads that slot after the current iteration's global write, it's safe to proceed without an extra sync. But we also have s_b being written in the next iteration. s_b is also per-thread slot. So no race. But what about the warp scan step? Warps read s_a[warp_id][lane]. That is, warp_id = tid/32. Since d_off = tid%32, t_off = tid/32, the warp read uses warp_id = t_off, lane = d_off. So warp 0 reads s_a[0][0..31], which corresponds to t_off=0, all d. These slots were written by threads with t_off=0, d_off=0..31. That's exactly the same warp (warp_id=0). Wait, threads with tid 0..31 have t_off = 0/32? No, tid 0..31: t_off = tid/32 = 0, d_off = tid%32 = 0..31. So warp 0 handles d=0..31? Actually thread 0..31 have same t_off=0 and varying d_off. They belong to the same warp (warp_id=0). When they do the warp scan, warp_id = tid/32 = 0. So they read s_a[0][lane] which corresponds to d_off = lane, t_off = 0. That was written by themselves! But what about warp 1 (tid 32..63)? They have t_off = 1, d_off = 0..31. warp_id = 1. They read s_a[1][lane] which corresponds to d_off=lane, t_off=1. Written by themselves. Good. So within a block, warp_id corresponds to t_off. Wait, that means each warp handles one t_off (one row of t) and scans across d? No! The warp scan is reading s_a[warp_id][lane] where warp_id = t_off, lane = d_off. So the values being scanned are for a fixed t_off and varying d? That would scan across d, not t! That's wrong. We need to be careful: we want each warp to scan along t for a fixed d. In shared memory, the preferred layout for reading a fixed d across t is s_a[d][t]. But our write layout used s_a[d_off][t_off] to avoid bank conflicts. Then d_off is the first index, t_off is the second. So for a fixed d (say d=0), the values across t are s_a[0][0], s_a[0][1], ..., s_a[0][31]. We want a warp to read these 32 values. So the warp should have lane correspond to t_off, and the warp should have a fixed d. That means the warp should consist of threads with the same d_off and varying t_off. But in our block, threads are grouped into warps by tid/32. tid = t_off*32 + d_off. So warp 0 has tid 0..31: t_off=0, d_off=0..31. This warp has varying d_off, fixed t_off. That's the opposite of what we need. We need to restructure the thread mapping. We want each warp to process one d (i.e., one channel) and 32 consecutive t's. So the 32 threads in the warp should have the same d_off and varying t_off. In terms of linear thread index tid (0..1023), we can map: d_off = tid / 32; // 0..31 t_off = tid % 32; // 0..31 Then threads in a warp (tid 0..31) have d_off = 0, t_off = 0..31. Good! Warp 0 handles d_off=0, warp 1 handles d_off=1 (tid 32..63), etc. There are 32 warps, each handling one d. Total threads = 32*32 = 1024. That's perfect. Now, for global memory load: we want coalesced loads. Threads in a warp have same d_off, varying t_off. So they load a[b, t_tile*32 + t_off, d_tile*32 + d_off]. Since d_off is same across warp, but t_off varies, the addresses are separated by D. That's strided! Not coalesced. So this mapping gives good warp scan but bad loads. We need a mapping where threads in a warp have contiguous global memory addresses for load, but after loading into shared memory, we can reorganize so that each warp reads contiguous values for its d. The standard approach is to use the "swizzle" mapping: - For loading: map threads so that consecutive threads have consecutive d_off. So d_off = tid % 32, t_off = tid / 32. This gives coalesced loads. - Store into shared memory in a transposed layout (or padded layout) such that the data is arranged by d then t. - For reading: we need warps to read data for one d across t. So warp_id should correspond to d, and lane to t. Since warps are tid/32, we could have a second phase where threads are remapped. But we can't change warp composition. However, we can read shared memory with a different mapping: each thread can read any element. If we want warp 0 (tid 0..31) to read d=0 across t=0..31, then thread i (lane i) reads s_a[0][i]. That means thread i reads the element for d=0, t=i. That works! The warp doesn't need to consist of threads with same d; it just needs to read the appropriate values. We can have each warp handle one d, and each thread in the warp read the element for that d and its lane as t. Since threads in a warp are tid=0..31, we can set: int lane = tid % 32; int d_for_warp = tid / 32; // but tid/32 is warp_id, so each warp has a unique d_for_warp. Wait, if tid 0..31: d_for_warp = 0. lane = 0..31. So warp 0 reads s_a[0][lane]. That's correct. If tid 32..63: d_for_warp = 1. lane = 0..31. Warp 1 reads s_a[1][lane]. Correct. So we can keep the block mapping as: For load: int d_off = tid % 32; int t_off = tid / 32; For warp scan: int warp_id = tid / 32; // 0..31 int lane = tid % 32; // 0..31 float a_val = s_a[warp_id][lane]; // read the d corresponding to warp_id, t corresponding to lane. This works! Because s_a[d][t] stores values for d across t. The load stored s_a[d_off][t_off] = a[idx] (with d_off = tid%32, t_off=tid/32). Then warp_id = tid/32 reads s_a[warp_id][lane] = s_a[tid/32][tid%32]? Wait, warp_id = tid/32, lane = tid%32. For tid 0..31, warp_id=0, lane=0..31. They read s_a[0][0], s_a[0][1], ..., s_a[0][31]. These were written by threads with d_off=0? Let's see: s_a[0][0] was written by thread with d_off=0, t_off=0. That's tid = t_off*32 + d_off = 0*32 + 0 = 0. s_a[0][1] was written by thread d_off=0? Wait, the write is s_a[d_off][t_off] = a[idx]. So s_a[0][1] is written by thread with d_off=0, t_off=1. That's tid = 1*32 + 0 = 32. So s_a[0][1] is written by thread 32, which is in warp 1. That's fine. s_a[0][31] is written by thread with d_off=0, t_off=31: tid = 31*32 + 0 = 992. That is warp 31. So all threads across the block contributed to writing d=0 column. Good. Then warp 0 (tid 0..31) reads s_a[0][0..31]. These values are all for d=0, t=0..31. Exactly what warp 0 needs. Similarly, warp 1 (tid 32..63) reads s_a[1][0..31]. These are for d=1, t=0..31. Good. So the mapping works. The load is coalesced because threads in a warp have same t_off and varying d_off (consecutive). The read for warp scan accesses different banks because s_a[warp_id][lane] is stored with padding [32][33], and warp_id is constant within a warp, lane varies. Banks = (warp_id*33 + lane) % 32 = (warp_id + lane) % 32. Lane varies, so no bank conflicts. After warp scan, each warp writes result back to s_a[warp_id][lane] (overwrite). Then __syncthreads(). Then for global write, we want coalesced writes. We need threads in a warp to write consecutive addresses. Consecutive addresses in output correspond to varying d and fixed t. So we need threads in a warp to have fixed t_off and varying d_off. That's the same as the load mapping. Since after __syncthreads(), s_a[d_off][t_off] holds the output for d=d_tile*32+d_off, t=tile*32+t_off. Wait, does it? After warp scan and write, s_a[warp_id][lane] = out_val for warp_id's d and lane's t. So s_a[d][t] = out for that d and t. Then if we have each thread read s_a[d_off][t_off] with d_off = tid%32, t_off = tid/32, it will read the output for its original d and t. Then write to global memory: out[b, t, d]. Since threads in a warp have same t_off and varying d_off, writes are consecutive in d. Coalesced! Perfect. So the kernel structure is: - tid = threadIdx.x - d_off = tid % 32 - t_off = tid / 32 - b = blockIdx.x - d_tile = blockIdx.y - d_global = d_tile * 32 + d_off - shared float s_a[32][33]; - shared float s_b[32][33]; - (maybe reuse s_a for output) Loop over tiles: t_global = tile*32 + t_off idx = b*L*D + t_global*D + d_global s_a[d_off][t_off] = a[idx] s_b[d_off][t_off] = b[idx] __syncthreads() // warp scan int warp_id = tid / 32; // 0..31 int lane = tid % 32; // 0..31 float a_val = s_a[warp_id][lane]; float b_val = s_b[warp_id][lane]; float ... warp shuffle ... float out_val = a_val * running_h + b_val; // write back to smem s_a[warp_id][lane] = out_val; // update running_h using lane 31 aggregate float chunk_a = __shfl_sync(..., a_val, 31); float chunk_b = __shfl_sync(..., b_val, 31); running_h = chunk_a * running_h + chunk_b; __syncthreads() // write to global memory out[idx] = s_a[d_off][t_off]; // optional __syncthreads() at end of loop to avoid next iteration load overwriting before all reads done? Implementation details: - L is assumed to be a multiple of 32. In the benchmark, L=4096, which is a multiple of 32. We can assert or just assume. - D is 256, a multiple of 32. Simpler: we can write a generic kernel that handles arbitrary D by processing full tiles of 32 and a remainder. For D not multiple of 32, the last tile has fewer than 32 d's. We should handle generically, but optimized for D multiple of 32 and L multiple of 32. However, the benchmark only tests with D=256, L=4096. But it's safer to write a generic kernel. Generic approach: process D in tiles of 32. For each block (b, d_tile), d_base = d_tile * 32. The block handles up to 32 d's, but we must check bounds: if d_base + d_off >= D, the thread should not load/store. In the warp scan, only warps with d_base + warp_id < D should participate; others can be inactive. But all threads in the block load/store anyway, with boundary checks. Let's design the generic kernel: __global__ void scan_kernel(const float* a, const float* b, float* out, int B, int L, int D) { const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int d_off = tid % 32; const int t_off = tid / 32; const int b_idx = blockIdx.x; const int d_tile = blockIdx.y; const int d = d_tile * 32 + d_off; __shared__ float smem_a[32][33]; __shared__ float smem_b[32][33]; float running_h = 0.0f; const int num_tiles = (L + 31) / 32; for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; ++tile) { int t = tile * 32 + t_off; bool valid = (b_idx < B) && (t < L) && (d < D); int idx = b_idx * L * D + t * D + d; // Load if (valid) { smem_a[d_off][t_off] = a[idx]; smem_b[d_off][t_off] = b[idx]; } else { smem_a[d_off][t_off] = 1.0f; // identity for a smem_b[d_off][t_off] = 0.0f; // identity for b } __syncthreads(); // Warp scan int warp_id = tid / 32; // 0..31 int lane = tid % 32; int d_scan = d_tile * 32 + warp_id; float a_val = 1.0f; float b_val = 0.0f; if (d_scan < D) { a_val = smem_a[warp_id][lane]; b_val = smem_b[warp_id][lane]; } // Only perform scan if this warp corresponds to a valid d if (d_scan < D) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float temp_a = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, a_val, offset); float temp_b = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, b_val, offset); if (lane >= offset) { b_val = a_val * temp_b + b_val; a_val = a_val * temp_a; } } float out_val = a_val * running_h + b_val; // Write output to smem_a smem_a[warp_id][lane] = out_val; float chunk_agg_a = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, a_val, 31); float chunk_agg_b = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, b_val, 31); running_h = chunk_agg_a * running_h + chunk_agg_b; } __syncthreads(); // Write to global memory if (valid) { out[idx] = smem_a[d_off][t_off]; } __syncthreads(); // needed for next iteration to avoid overwriting smem before all threads read } } Wait, the if (d_scan < D) inside the loop means some warps are idle. That's fine. The __syncthreads() must be called by all threads, which it is. The running_h is per warp (register). For invalid warps, running_h is unused. But there is a problem: the output write reads smem_a[d_off][t_off]. This will read the output written by the warp scan. However, if d_scan = d_tile*32 + warp_id >= D, then that warp didn't write to smem_a[warp_id][lane]. So for invalid d, smem_a contains the identity values (1,0) from load. But if the output for invalid d is not written to global memory, it's fine. However, valid threads might read smem_a[d_off][t_off] where d_off corresponds to an invalid d if d_tile*32 + d_off >= D. But we guard the global write with valid. So it's okay. But there is a subtle issue with the __syncthreads() at the end of the loop. In the next iteration, we overwrite smem_a[d_off][t_off] with new a values. Since all threads in the block participate in the load, and the previous iteration's global write is guarded by valid, some invalid threads might proceed to the next load immediately while valid threads are still writing global memory? No, all threads reach the __syncthreads() at the end of the loop before proceeding. So after that sync, it's safe to overwrite smem. Wait, the last __syncthreads() in the loop ensures all threads have finished writing global memory before any thread starts the next iteration. Good. However, there is another subtlety: in the warp scan, we have: if (d_scan < D) { ... smem_a[warp_id][lane] = out_val; ... } __syncthreads(); If d_scan >= D, the warp does not write to smem_a. But other warps might write, and then all threads read smem_a for global write. For valid d, the write happened. For invalid d, the smem still holds identity from load. Since we don't write to global for invalid d, it's fine. One more issue: in the generic kernel, t can go up to L. For the last tile, t may exceed L for some t_off. We set smem to identity for invalid t. In the warp scan, we read smem_a[warp_id][lane] for all lane 0..31. If t >= L for some lane, we loaded identity (1,0). The warp scan will include these identity elements at the end. Does that affect the scan? Yes, because inclusive scan over the tile includes elements beyond L, which would corrupt the prefix for the valid elements. We need to handle the last tile correctly. For the last tile, only the first (L % 32) elements are valid. The rest should be treated as identity so that they don't affect the prefix of valid elements. However, in an inclusive scan, if the invalid elements are at the end of the tile and have identity (1,0), then including them after the valid elements will not change the prefix for the valid elements because identity is (1,0): combine(valid_prefix, identity) = (1*valid_a, 1*valid_b + 0) = (valid_a, valid_b). So the prefix for the last valid element is unchanged. For elements after the valid range (invalid), we don't care because they won't be written to output (valid flag false). So loading identity for invalid elements is correct. Wait, need to check: the warp scan is inclusive. The elements are ordered by lane 0..31. If t goes from tile*32 to tile*32+31, and only the first M are valid, then lanes 0..M-1 have real data, lanes M..31 have identity. The inclusive scan will compute prefix for lane M-1 as combination of 0..M-1. For lane M, it combines prefix up to M-1 with identity, resulting in same prefix. For lane M+1, same, etc. So the prefix for valid lanes is correct. The invalid lanes get the same prefix as the last valid lane, but since we don't write them, it's fine. However, the chunk aggregate (lane 31) for the last tile will be the prefix up to lane 31, which is same as prefix up to M-1 (since rest are identity). So chunk_agg is correct. running_h update is correct. Great. Similarly for D: if a warp corresponds to an invalid d (d_scan >= D), we load identity and skip scan, leaving smem_a with identity. But we don't write output. And other warps are unaffected. Good. Now, what about the first tile? running_h = 0. The first element (t=0) should output b_0. Let's verify with identity: smem for d=0, t=0..31: lane 0: a_0, b_0. lanes 1..31: a_1..a_31, b_1..b_31. Warp scan inclusive: lane 0: a_0, b_0. out = a_0*0 + b_0 = b_0. lane 1: a_1*a_0, a_1*b_0+b_1. out = (a_1*a_0)*0 + ... = a_1*b_0+b_1. Correct. running_h after tile = chunk_agg_b (lane 31) = h_31. Correct. Now, about block/grid dimensions. Grid: dim3(B, (D+31)/32). For D=256, that's (32,8). Blocks = 256. Each block 1024 threads. Total threads = 262144. Warps = 8192. Shared memory per block: 2 * 32*33 * 4 = ~8448 bytes. That's well within limits. Register usage: each thread holds a few floats: a_val, b_val, running_h, temp vars. Probably < 32 registers. Occupancy will be high. Compute: O(B*L*D) work. Memory fully coalesced. Should be very fast. One concern: using two __syncthreads() per tile (128 tiles) = 256 syncs per block. Is that a lot? On A100, __syncthreads() cost is small (~10-20 cycles) if all warps are resident. With 32 warps per block, it's okay. But 256 syncs * 256 blocks * maybe some overhead. Still, with high occupancy, memory bandwidth dominates. Could we do the warp scan without storing back to shared memory and then reading again for output? If we write output directly to global memory from each warp, the writes would be strided (since warp writes out for fixed d and varying t). That is bad. The extra shared memory step ensures coalesced writes. We could also have each warp write its output to a temporary buffer in global memory with stride D, and then have a separate transpose kernel. But that adds another kernel and extra memory traffic. The fused approach is better. Alternatively, we could store output in registers, then use warp shuffle to exchange data among warps? No, warp shuffle only within a warp. We need to transpose data across warps to write coalescedly. Shared memory is the right tool. Another optimization: we can avoid the second __syncthreads() inside the loop by using double buffering in shared memory. But 128 iterations is fine. Each iteration does: - 2 loads (a,b) per thread from global memory (coalesced). - 2 stores to shared memory. - __syncthreads() - 1 load from shared memory per thread. - 5 shfl_up + 5 shfl (broadcast) + arithmetic. - 1 store to shared memory. - __syncthreads() - 1 load from shared memory per thread. - 1 store to global memory (coalesced). - __syncthreads() Actually, the first load from global and store to shared is done by all threads. Then __syncthreads(). Then warp scan loads from shared. Then writes to shared. Then __syncthreads(). Then loads from shared and writes to global. Then __syncthreads(). reasoning
Let me analyze the problem. I need to implement a parallel associative scan (linear recurrence) on GPU. The recurrence is: ``` h_0 = b_0 h_t = a_t * h_{t-1} + b_t for t > 0 ``` This is a classic prefix scan that can be parallelized using Blelloch scan or similar approaches. For the associative scan, we need to define an associative binary operator. For two operations at positions i and j (where j comes after i): - If we have `(a_i, b_i)` and `(a_j, b_j)`, the combined operation is: - `a_combined = a_j * a_i` - `b_combined = a_j * b_i + b_j` This is because: `h_j = a_j * h_i + b_j = a_j * (a_i * h_{i-1} + b_i) + b_j = (a_j * a_i) * h_{i-1} + (a_j * b_i + b_j)` So the binary operator is: `(a2, b2) ∘ (a1, b1) = (a2 * a1, a2 * b1 + b2)` Wait, let me be careful about ordering. If we process elements in order, and we want to combine segment [0:i] with [i:j]: - Segment [0:i] is represented by `(a_left, b_left)` where applying to initial state gives `h_i = a_left * h_{-1} + b_left` (with `h_{-1} = 0`, but `h_0 = b_0`). - Actually, let's think of each element as an affine transformation: `f_t(x) = a_t * x + b_t`. - The composition is: `f_j(f_i(x)) = a_j * (a_i * x + b_i) + b_j = (a_j * a_i) * x + (a_j * b_i + b_j)`. - So the composed transformation is `(a_j * a_i, a_j * b_i + b_j)`. For a scan, we want to apply this composition left-to-right. A common efficient parallel scan on GPU uses a two-pass approach (Blelloch or Hillis-Steele), or a block-based scan with shared memory. For good performance on H100, I should use a work-efficient parallel scan implemented in CUDA. Key considerations: 1. The sequence length is 4096 2. Batch size is 32, d_model is 256 3. Total elements: 32 * 4096 * 256 = 33,554,432 This is a moderate problem size. The main challenge is that we have B*D independent scans (32*256 = 8192 scans), each of length 4096. Since each scan is relatively short (4096), but there are many of them (8192), we have good parallelism. Strategy: - Launch one block per scan (one per channel per batch), so 8192 blocks. - Each block handles a sequence of length 4096. - Use shared memory for the scan within a block. - Since 4096 is relatively small, we can do a synchronous scan within a block. For a block scan with 4096 elements: - Load data into shared memory - Perform parallel reduction scan - Write back Actually, 4096 elements per block might be too many threads. Let's think about thread count. - With 256 threads per block, each thread handles 16 elements (4096/256 = 16). - We can use a sequential scan per thread on 16 elements, then a parallel scan on the 256 partial results, then add back. This is the classic "double-buffered" or "chunked" scan: 1. Each thread sequentially scans its 16 elements, producing a local result and a local aggregate `(a_local, b_local)`. 2. Do a parallel block-level scan on the 256 local aggregates. 3. Each thread combines its local scan with the prefix aggregate from previous threads. For the block-level scan on 256 items with 256 threads: - Use the Kogge-Stone (Hillis-Steele) scan or Brent-Kung ( Blelloch) scan. - With 256 threads and 256 aggregates, Kogge-Stone is simple: log2(256) = 8 steps. - Brent-Kung is more efficient (2*log2(n) - 1 steps, but with better lane utilization). Actually, for our binary operator, the scan is: ```cuda struct Pair { float a; float b; }; __device__ Pair op(Pair x, Pair y) { // Combine: first apply x, then y // y ∘ x return {y.a * x.a, y.a * x.b + y.b}; } ``` Wait, careful. In a scan, we usually want `scan[i] = op(elements[0], elements[1], ..., elements[i])`. If each element is an affine transform `(a_i, b_i)`, and composing `f_j ∘ f_i` gives `(a_j * a_i, a_j * b_i + b_j)`, then: - `scan[0] = (a_0, b_0)` - `scan[1] = (a_1 * a_0, a_1 * b_0 + b_1)` - `scan[2] = (a_2 * (a_1 * a_0), a_2 * (a_1 * b_0 + b_1) + b_2)` Let's verify: if initial state is `h_{-1} = 0`, then: - `h_0 = a_0 * 0 + b_0 = b_0` ✓ - `h_1 = a_1 * h_0 + b_1 = a_1 * b_0 + b_1` ✓ So the scan gives us `(A_i, B_i)` where `h_i = A_i * 0 + B_i = B_i`. So warp 0 handles channels 0-3, warp 1 handles channels 4-7, etc. Wait, this doesn't help because a warp's threads need to access shared memory without bank conflicts. Shared memory layout `smem_a[8][32]`: - Thread in warp w accesses `smem_a[dt][dd]`. - If warp w loads all 32 channels for dt=w (as in the original layout), then threads 0..31 access `smem_a[w][0..31]`. These are 32 consecutive words in the same row → bank conflicts! Because shared memory is banked by 32-bit words, and consecutive words are in different banks. So no bank conflict for a row access! Actually, accessing 32 consecutive 32-bit words maps to 32 different banks. No conflicts. But for the scan, thread dd needs to access `smem_a[0][dd]`, `smem_a[1][dd]`, etc. These are in the same column, separated by 32 words (the row pitch). `smem_a[dt][dd]` and `smem_a[dt+1][dd]` are 32 words apart. Since there are 32 banks, these map to the SAME bank! So all 32 threads in a warp accessing column dd for different dt values would cause 32-way bank conflicts. So `smem_a[8][32]` has column-major access pattern causing bank conflicts. Solution: Pad the shared memory. `smem_a[8][33]` or `smem_a[8][36]`. Then `smem_a[dt][dd]` and `smem_a[dt+1][dd]` are 33 or 36 words apart, avoiding bank conflicts. With padded shared memory and `smem_a[8][33]`: - Thread dd accesses `smem_a[0][dd]`, `smem_a[1][dd]`, etc. - These are at offsets `0*33 + dd = dd`, `1*33 + dd`, `2*33 + dd`, etc. - Offset difference = 33, which is not a multiple of 32. So bank(dd) != bank(dd + 33). No conflicts. Actually, in PyTorch eager mode: ```python for t in range(L): h = a[:, t] * h + b[:, t] outputs.append(h) ``` This involves: 1. `a[:, t]` indexing: cheap, returns a view 2. `a[:, t] * h`: element-wise mul, launches a kernel 3. `+ b[:, t]`: element-wise add, might be fused with mul if using NNC or if PyTorch uses a fused mul-add kernel. In eager mode without JIT, it's typically separate kernels or one fused kernel. 4. `outputs.append(h)`: stores a reference, no copy If PyTorch uses ATen for broadcasting element-wise ops, `a[:, t] * h + b[:, t]` probably compiles to a single element-wise kernel that reads `a[:, t]`, `h`, `b[:, t]` and writes to a new `h`. Let me reconsider the parallel scan per block with the tile-based transpose idea. The key insight from cutlass and modern scan kernels is to do a tiling where you load data in a coalesced way, then transpose in shared memory. For our problem: - We have [B, L, D] data. - We want to scan along L for each (B, D) independently. - The memory layout makes scanning along L strided by D. If we create a kernel where a block of 256 threads works on a tile of [B_tile, L_tile, D_tile], and loads data in a coalesced way... Actually, the most common GPU scan implementation (like the one in CUB or thrust) for segmented scan or array-of-scans typically assumes the scan dimension is the innermost (contiguous) dimension. If it's not, they might transpose first. For our case, maybe the simplest high-performance solution is: 1. Write a kernel that transposes `a` from [B, L, D] to [B, D, L] (contiguous along L) 2. Write a kernel that does parallel scan on [B, D, L] with contiguous L 3. Transpose output back from [B, D, L] to [B, L, D] Wait, but PyTorch's permute/reshape doesn't copy. We need an actual transpose copy kernel. Transpose [B, L, D] → [B, D, L]: - This is a standard matrix transpose for each batch element. - Each batch element is L×D = 4096×256 = 1M elements = 4MB. - 32 batch elements = 128MB per tensor. - Two tensors = 256MB. - Transpose bandwidth is typically 70-90% of peak. - Time: ~100-150us. Then scan on contiguous data [B, D, L]: - For each (b, d), scan over L=4096 contiguous elements. - We can use a very efficient parallel scan kernel. - With B*D = 8192 scans, each of 4096 elements. - CUB-style block scan with 256 threads per block can scan 4096 elements per block (16 elements per thread). - Blocks: 8192. Very high occupancy. - Time: For well-optimized scan on contiguous data, maybe 50-100us. Then transpose output back: - Another 100-150us. Total: ~300-400us. Very fast! The overhead of parallel scan can dominate for small arrays. Realistically, maybe 10-20us. So for Pass 2 with sequential scan on 1 thread per channel, the time is negligible (~20us). Pass 1: 512 blocks of 256 threads, each thread scans 256 timesteps sequentially. - Coalesced memory access. - Total memory: read a (~128MB), read b (~128MB), write local (~128MB), write aggregates (~1MB). - Time: With good memory utilization, maybe 400-500us. Pass 3: 512 blocks of 256 threads, applying prefix aggregates. - Read local results (~128MB), read prefixes (~1MB), write final (~128MB). - Time: ~300-400us. Total: ~800us + overhead. That's competitive with the transpose approach and might be simpler to implement. But wait, can we do Pass 1 and Pass 3 in fewer passes or combine them? Actually, note that in Pass 1, each block writes the local scan results AND the aggregate. In Pass 3, we read the local scan results and the prefix aggregate, then write the final result. Can we avoid writing local results to global memory and keep them in shared memory while also doing the prefix application? Not easily, because the prefix from Pass 2 isn't available until all blocks finish Pass 1. However, if we use a persistent kernel or cooperative groups, we might be able to do more. But that's overkill. Let me reconsider whether a single-pass parallel scan might be better after all. For a parallel scan of 4096 elements per scan with 8192 scans: - We can use 8192 blocks, 256 threads each. - Each block does a parallel scan of 4096 elements. - Total threads: 2,097,152 (good occupancy). - Memory access is strided (bad). But what if we use a very clever memory access pattern? What if we use `float4` and the compiler does some magic? No. What about texture memory or __ldg (load global through read-only cache)? The read-only data cache (L2) still loads cache lines. The bandwidth waste (31/32) is still there. But L2 bandwidth is high. Actually, let me compute: with L2 bandwidth of 3.35 TB/s, and loading 128MB of useful data but wasting 31/32 of each cache line, we need 128MB * 32 = 4GB of L2 bandwidth. At 3.35 TB/s, that's 1.2ms. Plus stores of 128MB * 32 = 4GB of write bandwidth, total 8GB at 3.35 TB/s = 2.4ms. Plus the fact that we only use 4GB out of the 8GB moved (the cache line waste). Still much faster than PyTorch. But with 2M threads active, can we sustain L2 bandwidth? The L2 is shared by all SMs. With all 132 SMs active, L2 bandwidth is 3.35 TB/s. With 2M threads, there are plenty of requests. Wait, for the parallel scan approach, the memory footprint per block is: - Load 4096 floats for a: 16KB - Load 4096 floats for b: 16KB - Store 4096 floats for output: 16KB - But due to strided access, each float load fetches a 128-byte cache line. So actual L2 traffic per block: 4096 * 128 * 3 = 1.5MB. - For 8192 blocks: 12.3GB of L2 traffic. - At 3.35 TB/s: 3.7ms. Hmm, that's not great. The sequential coalesced approach only moves 384MB total, which is much less. So despite lower occupancy, the sequential coalesced approach is more bandwidth-efficient and might actually be faster! For sequential coalesced: - Total data moved: 384MB (all useful, no waste) - Time at 500 GB/s (conservative with 4-8 SMs active): 768us. - Time at 1 TB/s (optimistic): 384us. For parallel scan with strided access: - Total data moved: ~12GB (384MB useful, 11.6GB wasted) - Time at 3.35 TB/s: 3.6ms. So sequential coalesced is likely 4-10x faster than parallel strided, despite using far fewer SMs. This is because coalesced access is SO MUCH more efficient. Now, can we improve the sequential coalesced approach to use more SMs and get closer to memory bandwidth saturation? We need more warps while keeping coalesced access. This means we need more blocks where each block's threads access consecutive memory. Since we have 32 batch elements and 256 channels, and coalescing works when threads in a warp access consecutive channels at the same timestep, we need warps that work on SAME batch, SAME timestep, DIFFERENT consecutive channels. In the sequential approach, each warp iterates over timesteps. At timestep t, warp w accesses channels (w*32) to (w*32+31) of batch b. These are consecutive. To get more warps active, we can have each block process MULTIPLE batch elements, and within each batch, have multiple warps processing different channel ranges. But with 256 threads per block: - Warp 0: batch 0, channels 0-31 - Warp 1: batch 0, channels 32-63 - ... - Warp 7: batch 0, channels 224-255 - Warp 8: batch 1, channels 0-31 - ... - Warp 15: batch 1, channels 224-255 This block has 512 threads (16 warps)! But CUDA max threads per block is 1024, and H100 can handle 1024. So a block of 512 threads is fine. With 512 threads, the block handles 2 batch elements. - Total blocks: 32/2 = 16 blocks. - Warps: 16 * 16 = 256 warps. - Still only 256 warps total. To get more warps, we need to process the SAME scan (batch, channel) with multiple warps, i.e., parallelize the scan of 4096 timesteps. But then we're back to needing parallel scan and its strided access issues. OR, we can process the sequences in smaller chunks where each chunk is assigned to a different warp, like the 3-pass chunked scan. In that approach: - Pass 1: 512 blocks of 256 threads, each thread handles 256 timesteps of one scan. Coalesced! Total warps: 512 * 8 = 4096 warps. - Pass 2: 32 blocks of 256 threads, each thread handles 16 aggregate values of one scan. Sequential but tiny data. Could use more blocks for this. - Pass 3: 512 blocks of 256 threads. Total warps: 4096. Total warps for the main passes: 4096 + 4096 = 8192 warps. That's 10x more than the simple 32-block approach! And the memory access in Pass 1 and Pass 3 is coalesced. The overhead of the extra passes is small because Pass 2 is tiny. Let me calculate the time for this chunked approach more carefully. Assume chunk size = 256 (16 chunks of 4096). Pass 1: - 512 blocks, 256 threads each. - Each thread processes 256 timesteps sequentially. - Per thread: 256 loads from a, 256 loads from b, 256 FMAs, 256 stores to local output, compute aggregate. - Per block: 256 threads * 256 timesteps = 65,536 timesteps = 65K elements. - Total blocks: B * (D/256) * num_chunks = 32 * 1 * 16 = 512 blocks. - Memory traffic: read a and b for all elements = 256MB, write local output = 128MB, write aggregates = ~1MB. - With coalesced access, effective bandwidth maybe 1-2 TB/s (using many SMs better now). - Time: ~200-400us. Pass 2: - 32 blocks of 256 threads. Each thread scans 16 aggregates. - Or to get more parallelism, use 256 blocks of 32 threads. - Actually, let's use 256 blocks of 32 threads. Each block handles 32 channels, one batch. - Memory: read 1MB, write 1MB. - Time: ~10us. Pass 3: - 512 blocks, 256 threads each. - Each thread reads 256 local outputs + 1 aggregate prefix, writes 256 final outputs. - Memory: read 128MB + 1MB, write 128MB. - Time: ~150-300us. Total: ~400-800us. This is better than the simple approach's ~600-1000us estimate. And it's relatively simple to implement. Can we do even better? What if chunk size = 128 (32 chunks)? - Pass 1: 32 * 1 * 32 = 1024 blocks. Better occupancy. - Each thread processes 128 timesteps. - Pass 2: 256 blocks of 32 threads, scanning 32 aggregates. More work in Pass 2 but still small. - Pass 3: 1024 blocks. Even more parallelism. That's a lot of sequential work per thread for Pass 2. A chunk size of 64 or 128 seems like a sweet spot. Actually, let me think about whether I can merge Pass 1 and Pass 2 more efficiently. But I want to see if we can do even better with warp-level reductions or shared memory within each chunk. For Pass 1, each thread handles 128 sequential timesteps. It computes: ```cuda float h = 0; float a_prod = 1; for (int i = 0; i < 128; i++) { int t = chunk_start + i; float av = a[b, t, d]; float bv = b[b, t, d]; h = av * h + bv; out_local[b, t, d] = h; // write local result a_prod *= av; } agg_a[b, chunk, d] = a_prod; agg_b[b, chunk, d] = h; ``` Wait, `a_prod` should be the product of all `a` values in the chunk. For the forward recurrence, this is correct. For Pass 2, for each (b, d): ```cuda float prefix_a = 1; float prefix_b = 0; for (int c = 0; c < 32; c++) { float av = agg_a[b, c, d]; float bv = agg_b[b, c, d]; // The aggregate of chunk c transforms h_in to h_out = av * h_in + bv // When chaining: prefix_{c} applied to h_{-1} gives prefix up to chunk c // new_prefix_a = av * prefix_a // new_prefix_b = av * prefix_b + bv prefix_a = av * prefix_a; prefix_b = av * prefix_b + bv; prefix_a_arr[b, c, d] = prefix_a; prefix_b_arr[b, c, d] = prefix_b; } ``` Wait, what exactly is the prefix we need for each chunk? If chunk c covers timesteps [c*128, (c+1)*128), and we want to apply the effect of all previous chunks to the local scan within chunk c. Let's define: - `local_h[c][i]` = result of scanning chunk c starting from h=0, at position i within chunk. - `agg_a[c]` = product of a over chunk c. - `agg_b[c]` = final result of scanning chunk c starting from h=0. If the input state to chunk c is `h_in`, then the output state is: - `h_out = agg_a[c] * h_in + agg_b[c]` For the scan, we need the input state to each chunk. Define: - `chunk_prefix[c]` = state before chunk c (i.e., after all previous chunks). - `chunk_prefix[0] = 0` (since h_0 = b_0, and the first element is in chunk 0). Actually, wait. The local scan of chunk 0 starting from 0 gives `h_0, h_1, ..., h_127` where these are the actual values if h_before_chunk0 = 0. But in the actual recurrence, h_before_chunk0 is well-defined based on prior elements. For chunk 0, there are no prior elements, so `h_before_chunk0` should be... hmm. Actually, the recurrence is: - `h_0 = b_0` - `h_t = a_t * h_{t-1} + b_t` For chunk 0 covering t=0..127, `h_before_chunk0` should be 0, because `h_0 = a_0 * 0 + b_0 = b_0`. So using h_in=0 for chunk 0 is correct! For chunk 1 covering t=128..255, `h_before_chunk1 = h_127` (the actual value). - `h_127_actual = agg_a[0] * 0 + agg_b[0] = agg_b[0]` - So `h_before_chunk1 = agg_b[0]` For chunk 2, `h_before_chunk2 = agg_a[1] * h_before_chunk1 + agg_b[1]`. In general, the prefix for chunk c is: - `prefix[0] = 0` - `prefix[c+1] = agg_a[c] * prefix[c] + agg_b[c]` And the actual value at position i in chunk c is: - `actual_h[c][i] = local_a[c][i] * prefix[c] + local_b[c][i]` where `local_a[c][i]` is the product of a from chunk start to position i, and `local_b[c][i]` is the local scan result at i starting from 0. Wait, I previously thought we only needed to store `local_h` (the local scan result). But to apply the prefix, we actually need to know the product of a up to each position. Hmm, if we have `local_h[c][i]` = scan result starting from 0, and `prefix[c]` = correct input state for chunk c, then: - `actual_h[c][i] = local_a[c][i] * prefix[c] + local_h[c][i]` But `local_a[c][i]` is the product of a from chunk start to position i. Do we have this? Actually, can we derive `actual_h[c][i]` from `local_h[c][i]` and `prefix[c]`? For a chunk starting at t_start: - `h[t] = a[t] * h[t-1] + b[t]` - Let `h[t] = local_h[t]` when `h[t_start-1] = 0`. - Let `h_actual[t] = a[t] * h_actual[t-1] + b[t]` with the true initial state. The affine transform from `h[t_start-1]` to `h[t]` is: - `h[t] = A[t] * h[t_start-1] + B[t]` where `A[t] = prod(a[t_start..t])` and `B[t] = local_h[t]`. So `h_actual[t] = A[t] * prefix + local_h[t]`. We need both `A[t]` and `local_h[t]`. If we only stored `local_h[t]`, we'd need to recompute `A[t]`. But we can store both, or we can store `local_h` and then for Pass 3, recompute or load `a` again. Options for Pass 3: 1. Recompute `A[t]` on-the-fly from `a`. This means loading `a` again and doing a sequential prefix product within each thread's 128 timesteps. That's additional memory traffic. 2. Store `A[t]` in Pass 1. This doubles the intermediate storage from 128MB to 256MB. 3. Note that `A[t] = a_prod_up_to_t`. We can compute this during Pass 1 and store it. Or we can compute it during Pass 3. Actually, wait. Let me think more carefully. During Pass 1, each thread scans 128 timesteps. It can compute and store the local scan result for each timestep. For Pass 3, to apply the prefix, it needs `A[t]` for each timestep. Option: In Pass 1, store only the final `local_h[127]` as `agg_b`. Don't store per-timestep `local_h` at all. Instead, in Pass 3, re-scan the chunk from scratch, loading `a` and `b` again, but this time apply the prefix. This means: - Pass 1: Scan chunk, compute aggregate, write aggregate to global memory. Do NOT write per-timestep results. - Pass 2: Scan aggregates to get prefixes. - Pass 3: Scan chunk again from scratch, applying prefix, write final output. This avoids storing intermediate per-timestep results! It does re-read `a` and `b` in Pass 3, but that's fine because memory bandwidth is the main resource. Wait, but we need the local scan without prefix to... no, we don't! We only need the final output with the correct prefix. In Pass 3: ```cuda float h = prefix_b; // prefix for this chunk // prefix_b is the correct state BEFORE the chunk starts // Actually, prefix_b should be applied as the initial state // So we scan the chunk starting from h = prefix_b instead of h = 0 for (int i = 0; i < 128; i++) { int t = chunk_start + i; float av = a[b, t, d]; float bv = b[b, t, d]; h = av * h + bv; out[b, t, d] = h; } ``` Wait, is this correct? - If prefix_b is the state before the chunk starts (i.e., after all previous chunks), then scanning this chunk starting with h = prefix_b gives the correct results. Yes! Because the recurrence `h = a * h + b` is the same regardless of initial state. So we don't need to store per-timestep intermediate results at all! We only need the chunk aggregates. This simplifies things: - Pass 1: For each chunk, scan sequentially, compute agg_a and agg_b. Write agg_a and agg_b to global memory. - Pass 2: For each scan, scan the chunk aggregates to compute prefix states for each chunk. - Pass 3: For each chunk, scan sequentially starting from the prefix state, write final output. Memory traffic: - Pass 1: read a (128MB), read b (128MB), write aggregates (1MB) - Pass 2: read aggregates (1MB), write prefixes (1MB) - Pass 3: read a (128MB), read b (128MB), read prefixes (1MB), write output (128MB) - Total: read a+b twice = 512MB, write output + intermediates = 258MB. Total: ~770MB. At 3.35 TB/s and good coalescing: ~230us. But realistically with overhead and not achieving peak, maybe 400-600us. And we get great parallelism with 1024 blocks in Pass 1 and 3, each with 256 threads. That's 524,288 total threads in the main passes. Excellent occupancy. Actually, we can further optimize by fusing the aggregate computation and having each block process multiple chunks or multiple scans in a tiled way. But let's also consider: can we avoid the second read of a and b by having a different structure? What if in Pass 1, we write the per-timestep results to a workspace, and in Pass 3, we read them and apply the prefix? Workspace size: 128MB for local results. Pass 3 would read 128MB workspace + 1MB prefix, write 128MB output. Total memory: Pass 1: 256MB read + 128MB write + 1MB write = 385MB. Pass 2: 2MB. Pass 3: 129MB read + 128MB write = 257MB. Total: 644MB. Compared to the recompute approach's 770MB, this saves about 126MB of reads but costs 128MB of workspace writes and reads. So it's roughly a wash. The recompute approach is simpler! No workspace allocation. However, there is another consideration: we could potentially overlap Pass 1 for some chunks with Pass 2 for others, or use a single kernel launch with persistent blocks. But for simplicity, let's stick with 3 kernel calls. Wait, can we at least combine Pass 2 into a smaller, more efficient kernel? For chunk size 128, num_chunks = 32. Pass 2 scans 32 aggregates per scan. With 8192 scans and each scan taking 32 steps, we can process this very efficiently. One option: launch 8192 blocks of 1 thread each. That's a lot of blocks, but each block has only 1 thread which is terrible. Better: launch 256 blocks of 32 threads. Each block handles 32 scans (one batch element's worth, but we only have 32 batches... wait, 32 batches * 256 channels / 32 scans per block = 256 blocks). Each thread in a block handles one scan (one channel), scanning 32 aggregates sequentially. That gives 256 blocks of 32 threads = 8192 threads. Blocks per SM: 256/132 ≈ 2. Threads per SM: 62. Occupancy: low, but the work is tiny. Another option: launch 1024 blocks of 8 threads. 1024 blocks is better distributed. Each block handles 32 channels of 1 batch? No, 8 threads can't handle 32 channels well. Actually, for 32 items per scan, we might be able to use parallel scan within a warp! - A warp of 32 threads can scan 32 items in parallel. - We have 8192 scans. Each warp scans 32 items for 1 scan? No, each warp has 32 threads. - If each thread handles 1 item, a warp can scan the 32 aggregates of ONE channel using Kogge-Stone in 5 steps. - 32 warps can scan 32 channels simultaneously. - 8192 channels / 32 = 256 blocks of 32 warps each = 1024 threads? No, 32 warps = 1024 threads. That's 256 blocks of 1024 threads. - Total threads: 262,144. Great! - But each block only processes 32 channels... For 8192 channels, 256 blocks. - Blocks per SM: 2. A block of 1024 threads = 32 warps. - Warp 0: channel 0, aggregates 0..31 (using 32 threads) - Warp 1: channel 1, aggregates 0..31 - ... - Warp 31: channel 31, aggregates 0..31 - So one block handles 32 channels. - But we need inter-warp communication to write results, and each warp operates independently. - Total blocks: 8192 / 32 = 256 blocks. Actually, the issue is that we only have 256 independent work units (32 batches × 8 groups of 32 channels). Unless we split batches further. What if a block handles 8 channels from 4 different batches? - 32 warps in a block. - Warp 0: batch 0, channel 0 - Warp 1: batch 0, channel 1 - ... - Warp 7: batch 0, channel 7 - Warp 8: batch 1, channel 0 - ... - Warp 31: batch 3, channel 7 - Block handles 4 batches × 8 channels = 32 channel-scans. - Still 256 blocks total. I think 256 blocks is the minimum for any workload that processes batch elements and channels together with 32 as a group size. Because 32 batches × 256 channels / 32 channels per block = 256 blocks. If we process 16 channels per block (half a warp per channel scan), we get 512 blocks. If we process 8 channels per block (quarter warp), we get 1024 blocks. If we process 4 channels per block, we get 2048 blocks. With 2048 blocks, we get 15-16 blocks per SM. That's much better! Actually, we CAN launch more blocks than the grid size I computed. I was dividing work as if each block had to handle a contiguous subset. But we can have each block handle a smaller subset, or even better, use a 3D grid. The total number of independent units to process in Pass 2 is 8192 scans × 32 chunk boundaries = 262,144 items. But these are not independent; they need to be scanned per channel. Since the scan per channel has 32 items and is independent, we have 8192 independent scans of length 32. We need to assign these to blocks. Standard CUB/thrust approach for batch scan: launch many blocks, each handling a portion of the batch. For 8192 scans of length 32, we can launch, say, 4096 blocks where each block handles 2 scans (2 channels) and does sequential scan on 32 items per scan. With block size 2 (1 thread per scan): terrible block size. With block size 64 (32 threads per scan, handling 2 scans): 64 threads. Blocks: 8192 / 2 = 4096 blocks. Each block uses 64 threads = 2 warps. Total warps: 8192. Blocks per SM: 4096 / 132 = 31. Threads per SM: 4096 * 64 / 132 = 1986. Near 100% occupancy! And memory access: each block loads 32 aggregates for 2 channels = 64 pairs = 512 bytes. With 2 warps, loads might be coalesced if arranged properly. Wait, if each block handles 2 channels, thread 0 handles channel 0 aggregate 0, thread 1 handles channel 0 aggregate 1, etc. That's a scan within a channel using multiple threads. Or thread 0 handles all 32 aggregates for channel 0 sequentially, and thread 1 handles all 32 for channel 1. Then at each aggregate position, thread 0 and thread 1 access different channels. If arranged properly, consecutive threads access consecutive channels, which is contiguous in the aggregate tensor. If `agg_a` is stored as `[B, num_chunks, D]`, then `agg_a[b, c, d]` and `agg_a[b, c, d+1]` are adjacent. So if thread 0 accesses `d=0` and thread 1 accesses `d=1` at the same chunk `c`, the accesses are adjacent and coalesced! Wait, but in a sequential scan, thread 0 iterates over `c=0..31` for `d=0`, then thread 1 does the same for `d=1`. They don't access the same `c` at the same time. So memory access within a warp is strided by `D * num_chunks * sizeof(float)`. If D=256 and num_chunks=32, the stride is 32KB! This is terrible. To fix this, we need the memory layout of aggregates to be something like `[B, D, num_chunks]` so that consecutive chunks of the same channel are adjacent. Then thread 0 accessing `c=0,1,2,...,31` for `d=0` accesses contiguous memory. If we store aggregates as `[B, D, num_chunks, 2]` (two values a and b), then thread 0 accesses contiguous memory for its scan. Thread 1 accesses a different contiguous block for `d=1`. Within a warp, threads 0 and 1 access different 128-byte ranges. This is fine - each warp accesses 32 cache lines (one per thread), each in a different address. The total range is 32 * 128 bytes = 4KB. But the lines are not contiguous (they're for different channels). The memory controller can handle this, but it's not perfectly coalesced. Actually, for the aggregate tensor, if we arrange as `[B, D, num_chunks, 2]` with `B` varying slowest and `num_chunks` varying fastest within a channel, then thread 0 accesses `base + 0*32*2 + chunk*2 + ab`, thread 1 accesses `base + 1*32*2 + chunk*2 + ab`. The addresses are separated by 64*4 = 256 bytes. Not contiguous, but within the same general area. A warp loads 32 cache lines, each separated by 256 bytes. This is somewhat scattered but better than 32KB apart. To make it coalesced, we'd need threads in a warp to access the same channel, different chunks. But then 32 threads would all be working on the same scan, doing a parallel scan. That brings us back to parallel scan. For simplicity, let's just store aggregates as `[B, D, num_chunks, 2]` and do sequential scan per thread. The aggregate data is tiny (1MB), so even with bad coalescing, it won't be a bottleneck. OK, let me now outline the kernel design I'll implement: **Aggregate Tensor Layout**: `[B, D, num_chunks, 2]` where the last dimension stores (agg_a, agg_b). **Pass 1: Compute chunk aggregates** - Chunk size: 256 (so 16 chunks for L=4096) - Grid: `dim3(B, D / 256, num_chunks)`? No, CUDA only supports up to 3D grid. - Better: `dim3(B * (D / CHUNK_THREADS), num_chunks)` where CHUNK_THREADS = 256. - Actually, if each block has 256 threads and handles 256 channels from one batch, then: - Grid.x = B = 32 - Grid.y = num_chunks = 16 - Each block: 256 threads handling channels 0..255 - Thread tid handles channel tid. - Total blocks: 32 * 16 = 512 blocks. - In the kernel: ```cuda int b = blockIdx.x; int chunk = blockIdx.y; int d = threadIdx.x; int t_start = chunk * 256; float h = 0; float a_prod = 1; for (int i = 0; i < 256; i++) { int t = t_start + i; float av = a[b * L * D + t * D + d]; float bv = b[b * L * D + t * D + d]; h = av * h + bv; a_prod *= av; } agg[b * D * num_chunks + d * num_chunks + chunk] = a_prod; agg[b * D * num_chunks + d * num_chunks + chunk + offset] = h; ``` Wait, the memory access pattern for `a` and `b` in this kernel: - Each block handles batch `b` and chunk `c`. - Threads 0..255 access channels 0..255 at timestep `t_start + i`. - These are contiguous! Consecutive threads (varying `d`) access consecutive memory addresses. - Per load instruction for a warp: 32 consecutive floats = 128 bytes = 1 cache line. - Coalesced! And we have 512 blocks. Each block does 256 iterations. Total threads: 512 * 256 = 131,072. Blocks per SM: 512 / 132 ≈ 4. Threads per SM: ~992. 50% occupancy. Decent. Actually, wait. 512 blocks × 256 threads = 131,072 threads. If each SM handles 2048 threads, that's 64 SMs worth of threads. With 132 SMs, we have plenty of room. 512 blocks / 132 SMs = ~4 blocks per SM. But each SM can hold up to 32 blocks. So we process 4 blocks per SM, then move on. All blocks start fairly quickly. Total active threads at once: 132 SMs × 4 blocks × 256 threads = 135,168 threads. That's basically all of them at once! Because 512 blocks / 32 blocks_per_SM = 16 SMs to hold all blocks? No wait, 512 blocks spread across 132 SMs means 3.88 blocks per SM concurrently. With 256 threads each, that's 992 threads per SM. So about half capacity. The other half of thread slots are empty. Is 50% occupancy enough? Probably yes. Each thread does 256 iterations with memory-bound behavior. 992 threads per SM generating memory requests should be enough to saturate a good fraction of memory bandwidth. **Pass 2: Scan chunk aggregates** - Input: `agg` of shape `[B, D, num_chunks, 2]` - Output: `prefix` of same shape - For each scan (b, d), we scan `num_chunks` aggregates. - Launch 8192 blocks of 1 thread each? No. - Launch 256 blocks of 32 threads. Each block handles 32 channels of one batch. - Grid: `dim3(B, D/32)` = `dim3(32, 8)` = 256 blocks - Block: 32 threads - Thread tid handles channel blockIdx.y * 32 + tid - In the kernel: ```cuda int b = blockIdx.x; int d = blockIdx.y * 32 + threadIdx.x; float prefix_a = 1; // Actually, the prefix starts as applying no chunks float prefix_b = 0; // h_before_chunk0 = 0 for (int c = 0; c < num_chunks; c++) { float av = agg_a[b, d, c]; float bv = agg_b[b, d, c]; // Compose: new_prefix = (av, bv) ∘ (prefix_a, prefix_b) // new_prefix_a = av * prefix_a // new_prefix_b = av * prefix_b + bv float new_a = av * prefix_a; float new_b = av * prefix_b + bv; prefix_a = new_a; prefix_b = new_b; prefix_a_out[b, d, c] = prefix_a; prefix_b_out[b, d, c] = prefix_b; } ``` Wait, I need to be careful about what `prefix` means. For chunk c, we want the state BEFORE applying chunk c. That is, the result of scanning chunks 0..c-1. - prefix for chunk 0: state before chunk 0 = 0 (no prior elements) - After processing chunk 0: state = agg_a[0] * 0 + agg_b[0] = agg_b[0] - prefix for chunk 1: state before chunk 1 = agg_b[0] - After processing chunk 1: state = agg_a[1] * agg_b[0] + agg_b[1] - prefix for chunk 2: state before chunk 2 = agg_a[1] * agg_b[0] + agg_b[1] In the loop: ```cuda float state_a = 1; // Applying state_a to h gives state_a * h + state_b float state_b = 0; // Initial state is 0 for (int c = 0; c < num_chunks; c++) { // state is the prefix before chunk c prefix_a[b, d, c] = state_a; prefix_b[b, d, c] = state_b; // Update state with chunk c float av = agg_a[b, d, c]; float bv = agg_b[b, d, c]; state_b = av * state_b + bv; state_a = av * state_a; } ``` Yes! This stores the prefix before each chunk, which is what Pass 3 needs. But wait, do we really need both `state_a` and `state_b`? Let's check. For Pass 3, we want to start scanning chunk c with the correct initial state. That initial state is the result of applying all previous chunks to h=0. Let's call it `prefix_h`. Actually, if we only store `prefix_h` for each chunk, is that enough for Pass 3? In Pass 3, we scan chunk c starting from `h = prefix_h`. We don't need `prefix_a` because `prefix_h` already encodes the combined effect. We just need the scalar value `prefix_h`. Wait, that's right! The prefix state is just a scalar float, not a pair. Because when we apply the previous chunks to h=0, we get a scalar value. So we only need to store `prefix_h` (state_b) for each chunk! Not state_a. Let me verify: - For chunk 0: prefix_h[0] = 0. We scan starting from h=0. Correct. - For chunk 1: prefix_h[1] = agg_b[0]. We scan starting from h=agg_b[0]. - First element of chunk 1: actual h = a[t1] * agg_b[0] + b[t1]. Correct. - For chunk 2: prefix_h[2] = agg_a[1] * agg_b[0] + agg_b[1]. - First element of chunk 2: actual h = a[t2] * prefix_h[2] + b[t2]. Correct. So `prefix_h[c] = result of applying chunks 0..c-1 to initial h=0`. The update rule is: ```cuda float prefix_h = 0; for (int c = 0; c < num_chunks; c++) { prefix_out[b, d, c] = prefix_h; prefix_h = agg_a[b, d, c] * prefix_h + agg_b[b, d, c]; } ``` We store `prefix_h` for each chunk. That's one float per chunk per scan. Memory for prefixes: 32 * 256 * 16 * 4 = 512KB. Tiny! This simplifies Pass 2 significantly. **Pass 3: Final scan with prefix** - Grid: same as Pass 1. `dim3(B, num_chunks)` with 256 threads per block. - Thread tid handles channel tid. - Load prefix for this chunk. - Scan chunk sequentially, starting from h = prefix. - Write output. ```cuda int b = blockIdx.x; int chunk = blockIdx.y; int d = threadIdx.x; int t_start = chunk * 256; float prefix_h = prefix[b * D * num_chunks + d * num_chunks + chunk]; float h = prefix_h; for (int i = 0; i < 256; i++) { int t = t_start + i; float av = a[b * L * D + t * D + d]; float bv = b[b * L * D + t * D + d]; h = av * h + bv; out[b * L * D + t * D + d] = h; } ``` Memory access: coalesced! Good. Total kernel launches: 3. Now let's think about whether we can do better. One optimization: instead of 3 separate kernel launches, can we combine Pass 1 for all chunks and then do the prefix propagation within the same kernel using shared memory or inter-block communication? Not really for general case. Another optimization: can we avoid writing the aggregates to global memory and instead use shared memory within a block? Not without processing all chunks of a scan in the same block, which would mean 16 chunks × 256 timesteps = 4096 timesteps per block. That might work! Wait! What if a block handles ONE scan (one b, one d) for ALL 4096 timesteps? But with 256 threads per block, we could have 256 blocks per batch element (one per channel). Total 8192 blocks. Each block handles 4096 timesteps using 256 threads in parallel! Or better yet: a block handles 256 channels from one batch for ALL 4096 timesteps. But with block size 256, and 4096 timesteps, that's 4096 timesteps / 256 threads = 16 timesteps per thread. This is the parallel scan approach, and memory is strided. What if the block first loads a tile of 256 timesteps for 256 channels into shared memory in a coalesced way, then processes it, then loads the next tile? Let's think about a streaming approach: - Block: 256 threads. - Handles: batch b, channels 0..255. - We process the sequence in tiles of, say, 32 timesteps. - For each tile: 1. Load 32 timesteps × 256 channels from `a` and `b` into shared memory. Coalesced! 2. Wait, we need to store 32 × 256 = 8192 floats per input. That's 32KB for `a` and 32KB for `b` = 64KB shared memory. Plus output. H100 has up to 228KB shared memory per block, so this fits. 3. Process the 32 timesteps: perform the scan for each channel using the state carried over from the previous tile. Processing the tile: - After loading, for each channel d (0..255), we need to scan 32 timesteps starting from the carried state. - We have 256 threads and 256 channels. We could assign each thread to one channel and have it sequentially scan the 32 timesteps. - But the data is in shared memory. Thread d accesses `smem_a[tile_t][d]` for t=0..31. - With `smem_a[32][256+padding]`, thread d accesses column d. Consecutive threads access different columns → no bank conflicts (each column is a different bank, and with padding if needed, horizontal accesses also have no conflicts). - Wait, `smem_a[t][d]` and `smem_a[t+1][d]` are separated by 256+padding. If padding=1, offset difference = 257, not multiple of 32. So no bank conflict. - Thread d scans 32 timesteps sequentially: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < 32; i++) { float av = smem_a[i][d]; float bv = smem_b[i][d]; h = av * h + bv; smem_out[i][d] = h; } ``` - Then write `smem_out` back to global memory. Coalesced! This is brilliant! It combines: - Coalesced global memory loads/stores (because we load/store tiles with consecutive channels accessed by consecutive threads) - Sequential scan within each thread (efficient, no synchronization needed) - Streaming through the sequence in tiles - Only ONE kernel launch! Memory traffic: - Read `a` once: 128MB - Read `b` once: 128MB - Write `out` once: 128MB - Total: 384MB. Perfect! No extra reads or writes. And with 32 blocks (one per batch element) and 256 threads each, total threads = 8192. But each block now processes 4096 timesteps in 128 tiles of 32 timesteps. Or maybe 64 tiles of 64 timesteps. Wait, 32 blocks is still only 32 blocks. Can we increase the number of blocks by having each block handle fewer channels? If each block handles 64 channels, block size = 64 threads. - Total blocks: 32 batches * (256/64) = 32 * 4 = 128 blocks. - Each block streams through 4096 timesteps. - Total threads: 128 * 64 = 8192. Same. If each block handles 32 channels, block size = 32 threads. - Total blocks: 32 * 8 = 256 blocks. - Total threads: 256 * 32 = 8192. Same. The total thread count is always 8192 because that's the number of scans. And each scan needs only 1 thread for sequential processing. BUT: we're now streaming tiles through shared memory. The tile approach makes the memory access coalesced. Actually, with the tile approach, each block does: - num_tiles = 4096 / TILE_T = say 128 tiles (for TILE_T=32) - For each tile: - Load 32*channels floats from a and b - Sequential scan of 32 steps - Store 32*channels floats to output - Total memory: 384MB, all coalesced - With 32 blocks, each accesses 12MB of data. Actually, the memory access pattern for loading tiles: - A warp loads `a[b, t_start + tile_t, d_start + 0..31]` for tile_t = 0..TILE_T-1. - We can load in various patterns. Let's think about the optimal tile load. We want consecutive threads to access consecutive memory. - Thread `tid` in the block handles channel `d_start + tid`. - To load the tile, we iterate over `tile_t` from 0 to TILE_T-1. - At each `tile_t`, all threads load `a[b, t_start + tile_t, d_start + tid]`. - These loads are coalesced and can be issued simultaneously for all threads in the block. - So for TILE_T=64, we issue 64 coalesced loads per block, each loading 256 floats. With 32 blocks, we're issuing 32 * 64 = 2048 coalesced loads total, plus the stores. This is very efficient! At each load, each block issues a request for 256 floats = 1KB. With 32 blocks, that's 32KB per load wave. At 2GHz and memory latency of ~400 cycles, to hide latency we need many waves in flight. With 64 loads per block, and assuming the hardware overlaps them, we might only have 1-2 waves per SM.
