Let me try both approaches: 32 blocks and 64 blocks. Actually, I'll implement a flexible kernel that can use any block size, and tune the TILE_T and channels_per_block. For simplicity, I'll use one block per batch element (32 blocks), 256 threads per block, and TILE_T=32 or 64. This is the simplest and most natural given D=256. Let me draft the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void scan_kernel(const float* a, const float* b, float* out, int B, int L, int D) { // blockIdx.x = batch index (0..B-1) // threadIdx.x = channel index (0..D-1) int b = blockIdx.x; int d = threadIdx.x; // Shared memory for a tile of TILE_T x D (but D is blockDim.x = 256) extern __shared__ float smem[]; // size = 2 * TILE_T * D float* smem_a = smem; float* smem_b = smem + TILE_T * D; float h = 0; for (int tile = 0; tile < L / TILE_T; tile++) { int t_base = tile * TILE_T; // Load tile into shared memory for (int i = 0; i < TILE_T; i++) { int t = t_base + i; smem_a[i * D + d] = a[b * L * D + t * D + d]; smem_b[i * D + d] = b[b * L * D + t * D + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Sequential scan within the tile for (int i = 0; i < TILE_T; i++) { float av = smem_a[i * D + d]; float bv = smem_b[i * D + d]; h = av * h + bv; out[b * L * D + (t_base + i) * D + d] = h; } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, the load loop: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < TILE_T; i++) { int t = t_base + i; smem_a[i * D + d] = a[...]; } ``` All 256 threads run this loop simultaneously. At each `i`, thread `d` loads `a[b, t_base+i, d]`. This is coalesced! Good. But we have `__syncthreads()` after the load and after the store. With 32 blocks, this should be fine. However, there's a problem: the `__syncthreads()` after the sequential scan is not needed for correctness within the block, because each thread only accesses its own `d` column in shared memory, and global memory writes are independent. BUT, we need to make sure all threads finish the store before we overwrite shared memory in the next tile. Actually, since each thread writes to a different global memory location, we don't need a block-wide barrier between tiles. We only need to make sure the shared memory loads for the next tile happen after all threads finish reading shared memory from the current tile. Since each thread finishes reading shared memory before writing to global memory, and the loads for the next tile don't depend on the previous tile's shared memory contents, do we even need `__syncthreads()` between tiles? Actually, yes we do. Without `__syncthreads()`, some fast threads might start loading the next tile into shared memory while slower threads are still reading the current tile. This causes data corruption. So we DO need `__syncthreads()` between tiles. Wait, actually, if each thread only reads column `d` and no thread ever reads another thread's column, then the shared memory slots for column `d` in the next tile are `smem_a[i * D + d]` which are the same addresses as the current tile (since `i` ranges 0..TILE_T-1 in both). If a fast thread overwrites `smem_a[0][d]` with the next tile's data while a slow thread is still reading `smem_a[0][d]`, there's a race. So yes, we need `__syncthreads()` between the sequential scan and the next tile's loads. Can we eliminate shared memory and just load directly? We COULD have each thread load directly from global memory: ```cuda float h = 0; for (int t = 0; t < L; t++) { float av = a[b * L * D + t * D + d]; float bv = b[b * L * D + t * D + d]; h = av * h + bv; out[b * L * D + t * D + d] = h; } ``` This is the simple approach without shared memory. It does coalesced loads and stores directly. No need for shared memory or barriers. Wait! Why do we need shared memory at all? The loads and stores are already coalesced. The sequential scan doesn't need shared memory because each thread operates independently. Let me verify memory access for the direct global memory approach: - Thread `d` accesses `a[b * L * D + t * D + d]` for varying `t`. - At a given `t`, threads 0..31 in a warp access `a[b, t, 0..31]`. These are contiguous. - The warp issues a single coalesced load for 128 bytes. - Perfect! And without shared memory, there's no need for `__syncthreads()`. The kernel is dead simple. So why did I think about shared memory? Because I was worried about cache performance, but for this access pattern, the L1/L2 cache should handle it well because consecutive warps in the block access the same cache lines (channels 0..31, 32..63, etc., at the same timestep). Actually, at timestep `t`: - Warp 0 accesses `a[b, t, 0..31]` → addresses `base + t*D + 0..31`. This is one 128-byte cache line. - Warp 1 accesses `a[b, t, 32..63]` → addresses `base + t*D + 32..63`. This is another 128-byte cache line. - ... - Warp 7 accesses `a[b, t, 224..255]` → 8th cache line. - Total for the block: 8 cache lines. And this happens for `a` and `b` (16 cache lines total). Then stores for `out` (another 8 cache lines). Total 24 cache lines per timestep per block. With 32 blocks: 768 cache lines per timestep. Each cache line is 128 bytes, so about 96KB loaded per timestep across all blocks. Over 4096 timesteps: 384MB. No waste! This is exactly the theoretical minimum memory traffic. And the loads are perfectly coalesced. So the simplest kernel is actually the best one! No shared memory needed. ```cuda __global__ void scan_kernel(const float* a, const float* b, float* out, int B, int L, int D) { int b = blockIdx.x; int d = threadIdx.x; float h = 0; for (int t = 0; t < L; t++) { float av = a[b * L * D + t * D + d]; float bv = b[b * L * D + t * D + d]; h = av * h + bv; out[b * L * D + t * D + d] = h; } } ``` With `gridDim.x = B = 32` and `blockDim.x = D = 256`. Now, the only concern is occupancy. With 32 blocks × 256 threads = 8192 threads, which is only 8192/2048/132 ≈ 3% of H100's thread capacity. BUT each block does a LOT of work (4096 iterations). And memory access is perfect. I think this might still be very fast for this problem size. Let me quickly think about if there are any compiler optimizations we can add: 1. `#pragma unroll` on the loop? 4096 is too large for full unrolling, but we can unroll by a factor. 2. Use `__restrict__` for pointers. 3. Use `float` consistently (inputs are fp32). Actually, let's estimate performance: - Per iteration per thread: 2 loads, 1 FMA, 1 store. - The loop has a data dependency: `h` depends on the previous iteration. - The compiler can pipeline the loads and FMAs using loop unrolling. - With `pragma unroll 4`, the compiler can interleave loads from iterations i, i+1, i+2, i+3. Let me add `#pragma unroll 8` to help with instruction-level parallelism. But there's a dependency chain: ```cuda h_0 = a_0 * h_{-1} + b_0 (4-6 cycle latency for FMA) h_1 = a_1 * h_0 + b_1 (waits for h_0) ``` Each FMA has ~4 cycle latency on Hopper. So iterations can't overlap much. The loop is latency-bound to the FMA chain. Wait, if the loop is FMA-latency bound, memory bandwidth doesn't matter! The FMAs can't be overlapped because of the dependency. FP32 FMA latency on Hopper is about 4 cycles. Throughput per SM is 128 FMAs per clock (with Tensor Cores, but for regular ALU... actually Hopper has 128 FP32 FMAs per SM per clock? No, that's too high. Let me think.) For Ampere, each SM has 4 warp schedulers, each can issue 1 FP32 FMA per clock, so 4 FMAs per clock per SM per warp, but overall the SM can issue up to 128 FP32 ops per clock? Actually, A100 SM can do 64 FP32 FMAs per clock (128 FLOPS). H100 is similar or higher. With a 4-cycle latency and 64 FMAs/clock throughput, the SM needs 4 * 64 = 256 independent FMAs in flight to saturate throughput. In our kernel, each thread does one FMA per iteration, and iterations are dependent. So each thread can issue one FMA every 4 cycles. To saturate the SM, we need 64 threads issuing FMAs every 4 cycles. With 256 threads per block, that's 4x more than needed. But the FMAs from different threads are independent! Thread 0's FMA at iteration i is independent of thread 1's FMA at iteration i. So the SM can interleave FMAs from all 256 threads. With 256 threads doing back-to-back dependent FMAs, and each FMA taking 4 cycles, the throughput across 256 threads is 256 FMAs / 4 cycles = 64 FMAs per cycle. This is exactly the SM's FP32 throughput! Wait, is that right? Each thread issues 1 FMA every 4 cycles (due to latency). With 256 threads, that's 64 FMAs per cycle. If the SM can do 64 FMAs per cycle, then we're compute-bound, not memory-bound! Actually, modern GPUs can issue multiple independent instructions per cycle per thread. If a thread has other work (like the next load) while waiting for the FMA, the scheduler might be able to overlap. But in our case, the loop is: ```cuda load a load b fma (depends on a, b, and previous h) store h ``` The loads can be pipelined. The FMA depends on the loads and previous FMA. The store depends on the FMA. With unrolling, we could have: ```cuda // iteration i load a_i load b_i // iteration i+1 (independent loads) load a_{i+1} load b_{i+1} // FMA for iteration i fma_i // store h_i store_i fma_{i+1} store_{i+1} ``` If the compiler can schedule this, loads from future iterations can overlap with FMAs from current iterations. But the FMA chain is still the critical path. Each FMA takes ~4 cycles, and we do one per iteration. Can we overlap FMAs from different iterations of the SAME thread? No, because of data dependency. Can we overlap FMAs from different threads? Yes, but they already run in parallel on different warp schedulers. Hmm, actually, with 256 threads = 8 warps, and 4 warp schedulers per SM Hopper, each scheduler handles 2 warps. At each cycle, each scheduler can issue an instruction from one warp. Actually, in this kernel, the critical path per thread is: 1. Load a (stalls ~400 cycles for L2 miss, but might hit L1) 2. Load b 3. FMA (stalls 4 cycles) 4. Store The loads at iteration i can be issued while the FMA from iteration i-1 is still in flight. This is where `#pragma unroll` really helps. If we unroll by 8: ```cuda #pragma unroll 8 for (int t = 0; t < L; t += 8) { float a0 = a[...t+0]; float b0 = b[...t+0]; float a1 = a[...t+1]; float b1 = b[...t+1]; ... float a7 = a[...t+7]; float b7 = b[...t+7]; h0 = a0 * h_prev + b0; h1 = a1 * h0 + b1; ... h7 = a7 * h6 + b7; out[...t+0] = h0; ... out[...t+7] = h7; h_prev = h7; } ``` With 8 loads from `a` and 8 from `b` happening first, the compiler can pipeline them. Then 8 dependent FMAs. But the dependency chain is still 8 FMAs long. This doesn't help latency hiding. Actually, unrolling doesn't help with the FMA dependency chain within a thread. Let me design it carefully. **Design for 3-pass approach:** **Pass 1: Compute chunk aggregates** - Chunk size: 256 - Grid: `dim3(B, num_chunks)` = `dim3(32, 16)` = 512 blocks - Block: 256 threads - Each block handles 1 batch, 1 chunk, 256 channels. - Thread `tid` handles channel `tid`. - Kernel: ```cuda __global__ void aggregate_kernel(const float* a, const float* b, float* agg_a, float* agg_b, int B, int L, int D, int chunk_size, int num_chunks) { int b = blockIdx.x; int chunk = blockIdx.y; int d = threadIdx.x; int t_start = chunk * chunk_size; float h = 0; float a_prod = 1; for (int i = 0; i < chunk_size; i++) { int t = t_start + i; int idx = b * L * D + t * D + d; float av = a[idx]; float bv = b[idx]; h = av * h + bv; a_prod *= av; } int agg_idx = b * D * num_chunks + d * num_chunks + chunk; agg_a[agg_idx] = a_prod; agg_b[agg_idx] = h; } ``` Wait, here the memory access for `agg_a` and `agg_b`: - Thread `d` writes to offset `d * num_chunks + chunk`. - For a warp of 32 threads with consecutive `d`, they write to addresses separated by `num_chunks` (16). So offsets are `chunk, chunk+16, chunk+32, ...`. Each address is 64 bytes apart (16 * 4). Not perfectly coalesced, but we can transpose the aggregate output. To make aggregate writes coalesced, we should store as `[B, num_chunks, D]` instead of `[B, D, num_chunks]`: - `agg_idx = b * num_chunks * D + chunk * D + d;` - Then threads 0..31 write to `chunk*D + 0..31`, which is contiguous. Coalesced! Let's use this layout: - `agg_a` shape: `[B, num_chunks, D]` - `agg_b` shape: `[B, num_chunks, D]` Actually, we can combine them: `agg` shape `[B, num_chunks, D, 2]` or two separate arrays. Two separate arrays are simpler. So Pass 1 writes: - `agg_a[b * num_chunks * D + chunk * D + d] = a_prod;` - `agg_b[b * num_chunks * D + chunk * D + d] = h;` And Pass 2 reads from this layout. **Pass 2: Scan aggregates** - Input: `[B, num_chunks, D]` for agg_a and agg_b - Output: `[B, num_chunks, D]` for prefix - For each (b, d), scan num_chunks aggregates. - We want to launch many blocks. Option A: one block per batch element, 256 threads. - 32 blocks. Each thread handles 1 channel, scanning 16 chunks sequentially. - Memory access: threads read `agg_a[b * num_chunks * D + c * D + d]`. - At a fixed `c`, threads 0..31 read contiguous memory. Coalesced! - Number of blocks: 32. Low. Option B: one block per channel group of 32, for each batch. - 32 * 8 = 256 blocks, each with 32 threads. - Thread `tid` handles channel `blockIdx.y * 32 + tid`. - Memory access: coalesced at each chunk iteration. - Better block count! ```cuda __global__ void prefix_kernel(const float* agg_a, const float* agg_b, float* prefix, int B, int num_chunks, int D) { int b = blockIdx.x; int d_group = blockIdx.y; // 0..7 int d = d_group * 32 + threadIdx.x; // 0..255 int base = b * num_chunks * D; float h = 0; for (int c = 0; c < num_chunks; c++) { int idx = base + c * D + d; float av = agg_a[idx]; float bv = agg_b[idx]; prefix[idx] = h; // prefix before applying chunk c h = av * h + bv; } } ``` Wait, the loop has `prefix[idx] = h;` followed by `h = av * h + bv;`. At each iteration `c`, the prefix stored is the state before chunk `c`. For chunk 0, prefix = 0. Correct. For chunk 1, prefix = agg_b[0]. Correct. This stores a scalar prefix per chunk per channel. Can we make this more parallel? We could process the sequential scan on 16 items with a warp, but 16 items is too small. Or we could have each block handle 32 channels and all 32 threads process them in a way that... no, sequential scan is fine for 16 items. Total work for Pass 2: 8192 scans × 16 items = 131,072 items. At 4 bytes each for read and write, about 1MB of traffic. Even with low occupancy, this will be very fast. With 256 blocks of 32 threads, total threads = 8192. Same as before, but much less work per thread. I think this is fine. **Pass 3: Final scan with prefix** - Grid: same as Pass 1. `dim3(B, num_chunks)` = 512 blocks, 256 threads. - Thread `tid` handles channel `tid`. - Load prefix for this chunk. - Scan chunk sequentially, write output. ```cuda __global__ void final_kernel(const float* a, const float* b, const float* prefix, float* out, int B, int L, int D, int chunk_size, int num_chunks) { int b = blockIdx.x; int chunk = blockIdx.y; int d = threadIdx.x; int t_start = chunk * chunk_size; int prefix_idx = b * num_chunks * D + chunk * D + d; float h = prefix[prefix_idx]; for (int i = 0; i < chunk_size; i++) { int t = t_start + i; int idx = b * L * D + t * D + d; float av = a[idx]; float bv = b[idx]; h = av * h + bv; out[idx] = h; } } ``` Memory access: coalesced! Now let me think about whether we can optimize further. For Pass 3, we read `a`, `b`, and `prefix`, then write `out`. `prefix` is only 1 float per thread. We can load it into a register. The main traffic is `a`, `b`, and `out`. For Pass 1, we read `a` and `b`, write `agg_a` and `agg_b`. One optimization: instead of writing both `agg_a` and `agg_b`, can we compute the prefix on-the-fly and avoid writing `agg_a`? No, because we need all aggregates to compute the scan in Pass 2. No, all threads in a warp execute the same instruction. If threads 0..15 load at `t=0` (chunk 0), threads 16..31 load at `t=0` (chunk 1, which is `t=256`). The warp issues a single load instruction, and threads 0..15 and 16..31 all load simultaneously. Memory access pattern for the warp: - Thread (0, 0): `a[b, 0, 0]` - Thread (0, 1): `a[b, 0, 1]` - ... - Thread (0, 15): `a[b, 0, 15]` - Thread (1, 0): `a[b, 256, 0]` - Thread (1, 1): `a[b, 256, 1]` - ... - Thread (1, 15): `a[b, 256, 15]` The first 16 threads access channels 0..15 at t=0. The next 16 threads access channels 0..15 at t=256. These are NOT contiguous! The first 16 addresses span 64 bytes. Let me draft the complete Python file: ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' __global__ void scan_kernel(const float* __restrict__ a, const float* __restrict__ b, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int L, int D) { int b = blockIdx.x; int d = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B || d >= D) return; float h = 0.0f; for (int t = 0; t < L; t++) { int idx = b * L * D + t * D + d; float av = a[idx]; float bv = b[idx]; h = av * h + bv; out[idx] = h; } } ''' cpp_source = ''' torch::Tensor scan_forward(torch::Tensor a, torch::Tensor b) { int B = a.size(0); int L = a.size(1); int D = a.size(2); auto out = torch::empty_like(a); scan_kernel<<<B, D>>>(a.data_ptr<float>(), b.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, L, D); return out; } ''' # Compile scan_cuda = load_inline( name="scan_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["scan_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, d_model: int): super().__init__() self.d_model = d_model def forward(self, a: torch.Tensor, b: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: return scan_cuda.scan_forward(a, b) ``` Wait, does `a.data_ptr<float>()` work? reasoning
It seems the cpp_source and cuda_source are being compiled separately and the CUDA kernel isn't visible from the C++ code. I need to put both in the same file, or use a single source. The `load_inline` function typically combines them. Let me check the proper way to do this. For `load_inline`, the `cpp_sources` should contain the host code, and `cuda_sources` contains the device code. But in order for the C++ compiler to see the kernel declaration, we need to provide it with the `extern "C"` declaration or a forward declaration. Actually, looking at the PyTorch docs, typically you put everything in a single string via `cpp_sources + cuda_sources`. The issue here is that the C++ compiler sees the .cpp file and doesn't know about `scan_kernel`. I should add a forward declaration for it in the cpp source, or include the kernel in a header that's included in the cpp source. Let me add an `extern "C"` forward declaration. Actually, for CUDA kernels, they have C++ linkage. I can declare them in the C++ source.

turn 4llm latency: 7.0s
reasoning
The issue is that cpp_sources and cuda_sources are put into separate files. The C++ file can't use <<<>>> syntax because it's compiled by g++ not nvcc. I need to declare the kernel and launch it with AT_DISPATCH or use a different approach. Looking at the torch.utils.cpp_extension documentation, the cpp_sources are compiled by the C++ compiler and cuda_sources by nvcc. So I need to put the kernel declaration in the cpp file, and keep the actual implementation in the cuda file. Or better yet, I can use torch.utils.cpp_extension.load with code containing everything in a single string or with an ATen wrapper. Let me look up the correct pattern. The proper way is to have a cuda function wrapper that is callable from C++. Like: In cuda file: ```cpp torch::Tensor scan_forward_cuda(torch::Tensor a, torch::Tensor b) { ... scan_kernel<<<...>>>(...); return out; } ``` And then in cpp file: ```cpp torch::Tensor scan_forward_cuda(torch::Tensor a, torch::Tensor b); torch::Tensor scan_forward(torch::Tensor a, torch::Tensor b) { return scan_forward_cuda(a, b); } ``` Or, alternatively, put the kernel declaration in the cuda file and expose a C++ wrapper. Let me just put all the actual work in the cuda_sources with a C++ function that calls the kernel. The cpp_sources can then declare that function. Actually, looking at examples, a common pattern is to put the kernel in the cuda source and also put the C++ wrapping function in the cuda source. Then the cpp source just contains the declaration.

This is memory-bound since each thread reads memory coalesced for D strides but not optimal for L. Let me think about better approaches: 1. **Memory Coalescing**: Currently, each thread accesses memory with stride D between timesteps. This is bad because neighboring threads access neighboring elements at the same time, but within a thread the accesses are strided by D*L or D. Actually, let me recalculate: - Thread d accesses idx = batch*L*D + t*D + d - At time t, threads d and d+1 access adjacent memory → coalesced! - But for a single thread, consecutive accesses are D elements apart → not cache friendly 2. **Better approach - Work per thread**: Each thread can process multiple D elements. Currently D=256 threads per block, which is fine. But we could process with vectorized loads (float4) to improve memory bandwidth. 3. **Parallel scan using shared memory**: We could use a parallel associative scan algorithm within a block. But for L=4096 and D=256, a basic sequential scan per thread might be close to memory bound anyway. Actually, let me think more carefully. The kernel reads 2 inputs and writes 1 output per element. Total memory traffic = 3 * B * L * D * 4 bytes = 3 * 32 * 4096 * 256 * 4 = ~402 MB per forward pass. At 3.35 TB/s, this would take ~120 microseconds just for memory. The reference PyTorch implementation does Python-level loops, so it's much slower. But can we do better? This means we only utilize 2/64 of each cache line for scalar data in L1, but L2 should be fine since it's sequential at the warp level across time. Split each (batch, d) scan of length L into chunks of size C 2. Each thread block processes one chunk, computing local prefix sums 3. Then compute the "carry" across chunks using another kernel or within the same kernel For the operation (a, b) ∘ (a', b') = (a'*a, a'*b + b'): - The local scan in each chunk gives (a_local, b_local) at each position - The cumulative effect of a chunk is: a_chunk = product of all a in the chunk, b_chunk = the final h value if starting from h=0 - Then we propagate the chunk carries across chunks This is the classic two-level scan. It allows parallel processing of chunks. For L=4096 and C=256, we'd have 16 chunks per (batch, d). With B=32, D=256, that's 8192 scans × 16 chunks = 131072 chunks. Let's think about the memory bound vs compute bound: - Read 2 * 32 * 4096 * 256 * 4 = 268 MB - Write 1 * 32 * 4096 * 256 * 4 = 134 MB - Total 402 MB - At 3.35 TB/s, this would take ~120 microseconds memory-limited - Compute: 32 * 4096 * 256 * 2 = 67 M FLOPs - At 67 TFLOPS, this would take ~1 microsecond compute-limited So this is memory bandwith limited! We need to maximize memory bandwidth utilization. The simple kernel does coalesced accesses but only uses 32 warps total across all SMs. On H100 with many SMs, we want more concurrent warps. With 256 threads per block * 32 blocks = 8192 threads, and each thread does 4096 iterations, that's plenty of work but not enough thread-level parallelism for all SMs. Wait, H100 has 132 SMs (I need to check). With only 32 blocks, many SMs are idle. We should use more blocks. So a better kernel would launch more blocks, each with fewer threads. For example, 256 blocks * 32 threads = 8192 threads, but spread across 256 blocks, giving much better occupancy. Or even: 8192 blocks * 1 thread each. Then each thread does a sequential scan of 4096 elements. With 8192 blocks * 1 thread = 8192 threads, but they can't all run concurrently. Actually, each block has overhead. We want ~128-256 blocks ideally. Let me try: 128 blocks (one per SM on average, or 2 per SM) * 64 threads = 8192 threads. This would give better occupancy. Or: 256 blocks * 32 threads = 8192 threads. For even better memory bandwidth: if each thread processes 32 channels sequentially (to amortize block overhead), we'd have 8192/32 = 256 blocks with 32 threads each, each processing 32 channels. Let me implement a kernel with more blocks for better SM utilization. Also, for fp32 with 256 channels and 32 batch = 8192 independent scans of length 4096: - If we use 1 thread per scan, we have 8192 threads total. - We should launch in blocks of e.g. 128 threads => 64 blocks. But for memory coalescing, adjacent threads should access adjacent channels. So each block handles a portion of (batch, channel), and threads in a block access adjacent channels.
